And the madman, and the drunkard. And the traveler and the one praying in the mosque in congregation, or in his house in a city, bring them (perform adhan and iqamah). And leaving them (the adhan and iqamah) is disliked for the first two, not for the third. (And the madman, and the drunkard): meaning it is disliked, and it is recommended (or some say obligatory) to repeat it. (And the traveler... etc.): meaning dislike of leaving each one of them for the traveler and the one praying in the mosque in congregation. Her adhan is disliked, and likewise the adhan of a hermaphrodite. As in al-Bahr. [1] His saying: 'the madman and the drunkard': and likewise the child who does not understand, and the idiot, because their adhan comes from one without understanding and choice, so it is not considered like the sound of a bird. As in al-Binayah and al-Bahr. [2] His saying: 'and it is recommended': and it is said that repetition is obligatory for the adhan of the madman and the drunkard. [3] His saying: 'the traveler': whether alone or with companions, due to the hadith of Malik ibn al-Huwayrith: When he wanted to leave from the Prophet (peace be upon him) to his homeland with Ibn Umar, the Prophet said: 'When the time for prayer comes, let one of you give the adhan' — narrated by the authors of the six books. [4] His saying: 'and the one praying in the mosque': if he prays alone in the mosque after the congregation has been held, it is disliked for him to do both (adhan and iqamah), as mentioned in al-Dhakhirah and others. [0] His saying: 'not for the third': because the one praying in his house, even if he prayed without adhan and iqamah, then...
Source pages p. 44

Commentary
This passage covers additional rulings on who should give adhan. First, the adhan of a madman (majnun) or a drunkard (sakran) is disliked (makruh), and it is recommended (mustahabb) to repeat it; some scholars even say it is obligatory (wajib) to repeat because their adhan lacks proper intention and understanding. This also applies to a child who does not understand and an idiot (ma'tuh). Their adhan is not legally significant, like the sound of a bird. Second, for a traveler (musafir) whether alone or with companions, it is established from the hadith that they should give adhan and iqamah. The hadith of Malik ibn al-Huwayrith is cited where the Prophet ordered one of them to give adhan. The ruling applies to both the traveler and the one praying in congregation in the mosque. Leaving the adhan and iqamah is disliked (makruh) for these two categories: the traveler and the one praying in congregation in the mosque. However, for a third category — the one who prays alone in his house in a city (after the congregation has been held in the mosque) — it is not disliked to leave the adhan and iqamah. The reason given is that the congregational adhan suffices for him. However, if he prays alone in the mosque after the congregation, it is disliked for him to give adhan and iqamah because they have already been performed.

Key Terms
المجنون (al-majnun): A person who has lost his intellect due to insanity.
السكران (al-sakran): A person intoxicated to the point of losing intellect.
المسافر (al-musafir): A person traveling a distance that permits qasr of salah (approx. 48 miles).

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
What is the ruling for a traveler regarding adhan and iqamah?
A) They are optional; he may leave them.
B) He should perform them; leaving them is disliked.
C) He must perform them; leaving them is forbidden.
D) Adhan is required but iqamah is optional.

Key Takeaway
The adhan of those lacking intellect is invalid; the traveler and congregant must perform adhan, but leaving it is disliked only for them.

10 min
Rulings on Adhan: When Leaving Adhan and Iqamah is Disliked vs Permissible
Ruling statement with proof and commentary

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain the Hanafi ruling on leaving adhan and iqamah for a traveler, congregant, and resident praying at home, and apply the hadith proof to distinguish their cases.

Core Idea
Leaving adhan and iqamah is disliked for a traveler and congregant, but permissible for a resident in a city because the public mosque's call suffices.

In this passage from 'Umdat al-Ri'aya, we learn that the obligation (or strong sunnah) of adhan and iqamah is situational. While a traveler and a person praying in a mosque in congregation must not omit them, a resident praying in his home in a city is excused because the public adhan suffices. The commentary also discusses the validity of adhan from an insane or drunk person, highlighting a nuance in Hanafi law.

Guided Path
1
Identify the three categories of people
The passage discusses three cases: a traveler, one praying in a mosque in congregation, and one praying in his house in a city. For each, the ruling on leaving adhan and iqamah differs.
Note that the first two are grouped together as 'الأولين' (the first two), and the third is separate.
2
Understand the ruling for the traveler
A traveler, whether alone or with companions, is commanded by the hadith to give adhan. Leaving it is makruh (disliked), but the prayer is still valid.
The hadith's imperative is interpreted as recommendation, not obligation, hence makruh tanzihi.
3
Understand the ruling for the congregant in the mosque
One who prays in a mosque in congregation should also not leave adhan and iqamah. But if he enters a mosque after the congregation has already prayed, repeating adhan is disliked; only iqamah is recommended.
The reason: repeating adhan causes confusion, but iqamah is for the current congregation.
4
Understand the ruling for the resident at home
A person praying alone in his house in a city may leave adhan and iqamah because the public mosque's adhan suffices. He is considered to have prayed with them effectively.
This is based on the practice of Ibn Mas'ud and the principle of kifayah (sufficiency).
5
Analyze the validity of adhan from an insane or drunk person
Their adhan is invalid because it lacks comprehension. It is recommended (or, by some, obligatory) to repeat it. The relied-upon position is that it is mustahabb.
This shows the importance of intent and volition in acts of worship.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
والمجنون، والسكران ويأتي بهما المسافر، والمصلي في المسجد جماعة، أو في بيته في مصر، وكُره تركهما للأولين لا للثالث : أي كره تركهما : أي ترك كلّ واحدٍ منهما للمسافر، والمصلي في المسجد جماعة كره أذانها، وكذا أذانُ الخنثى. كما في «البحر». قوله : والمجنون والسكران ؛ وكذا الصبيُّ الذي لا يعقل، والمعتوه ؛ لأنّ أذانهم صادر من غير فهم واختيار فلا يعتد به كصوت الطير. كذا في «البناية»، و«البحر». قوله : ويستحب ؛ وقيل : تجب الإعادة في أذان المجنون والسكران. قوله : المسافر؛ منفرداً كان أو مع الرفقاء ؛ لحديث مالك بن الحويرث : إنه لما أراد الانصراف من عند النبي الله إلى وطنه ومعه ابن عمر الله قال له رسول الله صلى الله عليه وسلم : «إذا حضرت الصلاة فليؤذن أحدكم» ، أخرجه أصحاب الكتب الستة. قوله : والمصلي في المسجد ؛ فإن صلى منفرداً في المسجدِ بعدما صلّي فيه فإنّه يكره له فعلهما ذكره في «الذخيرة»، وغيره. قوله : لا للثالث ؛ لأنَّ المصلِّي في بيته وإن كان صلى بغير أذان وإقامة ، فإنّه صلى بهما حكماً ؛ لكفاية أذان مسجدِ حَيّه ، بخلاف المسافر فإنه لو تركهما يكون مُصلياً بدون أذان وإقامة حقيقة وحكماً. كذا في «الذخيرة». قوله : ترك واحد منهما ؛ أي واحد كان، فإنَّ الأذان والإقامة جعلا من شعائرِ الإسلام الجماعات المساجد، فيكره فيها ترك كل منهما ، فإن دخل مع رفقائه في مسجد قد صلّى فيه بأذان وإقامة وصلّى مع الجماعة لم يؤدِّن، ولا بأس بالإقامة، بل هو الأفضلُ بناءً على أن تكرار الآذان في وقت واحدٍ مشوّش، والإقامة للحاضرين وهم في الجماعة الثانية غير الأولين، فينبغي لهم الإقامة. قوله : لقول ابن مسعود الله؛ «فإنَّه صلّى مع علقمة بن قيس، والأسود بن يزيد بغير أذان وإقامة ، وقال : يجزئ إقامة الناس حولنا»)، أخرجه محمد في كتاب «الآثار»، وأما اللفظ الذي ذكره الشارح أخذاً من «الهداية، فلم أجده. قوله : وهذا ؛ أي جواز ترك كل منهما للمصلي في بيته.
And the insane, and the drunk, and the traveler brings them both, and the one praying in the mosque in congregation, or in his house in a city, and it is disliked to leave them both for the first two, not for the third. That is: it is disliked to leave them both, i.e., to leave each one of them for the traveler and the one praying in the mosque in congregation; it is disliked her adhan, and likewise the adhan of the khuntha. As in 'Al-Bahr'. He said: the insane and the drunk; and likewise the child who does not understand, and the imbecile; because their adhan issues without understanding and choice, so it is not considered, like the sound of a bird. As in 'Al-Binaya' and 'Al-Bahr'. He said: and it is recommended, and it is said: repetition is obligatory in the adhan of the insane and the drunk. He said: the traveler; whether alone or with companions; due to the hadith of Malik ibn al-Huwayrith: When he wanted to depart from the Prophet (SAW) to his homeland, and with him was Ibn Umar (RA), the Messenger of Allah (SAW) said to him: 'When the prayer is due, let one of you give the adhan.' Reported by the authors of the six books. He said: and the one praying in the mosque; then if he prays alone in the mosque after it has been prayed in, then it is disliked for him to do them both; mentioned in 'Al-Dhakhira' and others. He said: not for the third; because the one praying in his house even if he prays without adhan and iqamah, then he prays with both effectively; due to the sufficiency of the adhan of his district's mosque, unlike the traveler, because if he leaves them both, he prays without adhan and iqamah actually and effectively. As in 'Al-Dhakhira'. He said: leaving one of them both; i.e., whichever one, because adhan and iqamah are made from the symbols of Islam for congregations in mosques, so it is disliked to leave each of them therein. So if he enters with his companions into a mosque that has been prayed in with adhan and iqamah, and he prays with the congregation, he does not give adhan, and there is no harm in iqamah; rather it is better, based on the fact that repeating the adhan at one time is confusing, and the iqamah is for those present, and they are in the second congregation not the first, so they should give iqamah. He said: due to the statement of Ibn Mas'ud (RA): 'He prayed with Alqamah ibn Qays and al-Aswad ibn Yazid without adhan and iqamah, and said: The iqamah of the people around us suffices.' Reported by Muhammad in 'Al-Athar'. As for the wording mentioned by the commentator taken from 'Al-Hidayah', I did not find it. He said: and this; i.e., the permissibility of leaving both for the one praying in his house.
Source pages p. 44

Commentary
This passage from 'Umdat al-Ri'aya, a commentary on al-Wiqaya, discusses the rulings of adhan and iqamah. It begins with the matn stating that it is makruh (disliked) for a traveler and one praying in a congregation in a mosque to leave both adhan and iqamah, but not for one praying alone in his house in a city. The commentary explains that the adhan of an insane person or a drunkard, as well as a child who lacks understanding or a mentally deficient person, is not valid because it issues without comprehension and volition; it is like the sound of a bird. Therefore, it is recommended (or according to some, obligatory) to repeat their adhan. For the traveler, whether traveling alone or with companions, the proof is the hadith of Malik ibn al-Huwayrith where the Prophet (SAW) commanded that one of them give adhan when prayer time comes. For the one praying in the mosque, if he prays alone in a mosque where a congregation has already been held, it is disliked for him to perform both adhan and iqamah because the mosque's adhan suffices. However, if he prays in his house in a city, he is considered to have prayed with adhan and iqamah effectively because the adhan of the nearest public mosque suffices for him, unlike the traveler who physically lacks any adhan or iqamah. The passage also clarifies that if one enters a mosque where a congregation has already taken place, he should not repeat the adhan but may give iqamah, as repeating adhan is confusing and iqamah benefits the present congregation. The commentator cites the practice of Ibn Mas'ud who prayed without adhan or iqamah in a gathering, relying on the congregation's iqamah. Thus, the ruling distinguishes between the traveler who must personally ensure adhan/iqamah and the resident who can rely on the public mosque's call.

Key Terms
الأذان (al-adhan): The call to prayer, consisting of specific phrases, announced at the beginning of the prayer time.
الإقامة (al-iqamah): The second call immediately before the prayer begins, similar to adhan but with additional phrase 'qad qamat al-salah'.
الكراهة (al-karahah): Legal dislike; in this context, it is karahah tanzihiyyah (light dislike) because the act is leaving a sunnah.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what is the ruling for a person who prays alone in his house in a city without adhan and iqamah?
A) It is makruh (disliked).
B) It is permissible (mubah).
C) It is haram (forbidden).
D) It is invalid (batil).

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi school distinguishes between travelers and residents: adhan is a personal sunnah for travelers but communal sufficiency exempts residents in cities.

Salah — Prayer
beginner

Leaving Adhan or Iqamah: Three Scenarios and the Proof
Ruling on omitting adhan/iqamah with proof from Ibn Mas'ud

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on omitting adhan or iqamah for a congregant in the mosque, a traveler, and a resident at home, and cite the proof from Ibn Mas'ud.

Core Idea
Adhan and iqamah are rituals; omitting them is disliked in congregation but permitted for a traveler or resident whose mosque suffices.

The adhan and iqamah are among the enduring symbols of Islam, especially for congregational prayer. But does every individual have to perform them? This lesson explores three distinct situations from the Hanafi school: praying in a mosque congregation, traveling, and praying at home in a city with a mosque. The ruling varies, and the proof comes from the practice of a great Companion, Ibn Mas'ud.

Guided Path
1
Distinguish the three scenarios
Identify the three cases: (1) mosque congregation, (2) traveler, (3) resident at home in a city. For each, note the ruling on omitting adhan and/or iqamah.
The ruling is not uniform; it depends on context.
2
Extract the hukm for each
In congregation: makruh (disliked) to leave one of them. Traveler: permissible to suffice with iqamah only (leave adhan). Resident: permissible to leave both if the quarter's mosque has performed them.
Makruh here is tanzih (not severely disliked).
3
Locate the dalil for the resident's case
The commentator cites the saying of Ibn Mas'ud: 'The adhan of the quarter suffices us,' implying that the mosque's call covers the resident who prays at home.
The proof is a hadith narrated by Muhammad in al-Athar.
4
Analyze the wajh al-dalalah
The operative part: Ibn Mas'ud omitted both adhan and iqamah when praying at home and explained that the call from the mosque around them was sufficient. This establishes that the resident may rely on the mosque's adhan.
The traveler is different because he is not in a locality where a mosque's call reaches him.
5
Note the exception: traveler cannot leave both
The text explicitly says the traveler who leaves both prays without adhan and iqamah literally and in ruling. Therefore he must at least make iqamah.
The ruling for villages is analogous to cities if a mosque exists.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

Book of Prayer / The Adhan. [1] As for leaving one of them (adhan or iqamah), he did not mention it. So we say: As for the one praying in a mosque in congregation, it is disliked (makruh) for him to leave one of them. As for the traveler, it is permissible for him to suffice with only the iqamah. As for the one praying in his house in a city if he leaves both, it is permissible due to the saying of Ibn Mas'ud: 'The adhan of the quarter suffices us.' This is if the adhan and iqamah are performed in the mosque of his quarter. As for in villages: if there is a mosque in it with adhan and iqamah, then the ruling of the one praying there is as previously mentioned: he prays with both in a ruling sense due to the sufficiency of the adhan of his mosque's quarter, unlike the traveler, for if he leaves both, he prays without adhan and iqamah both literally and in ruling. Thus in al-Dhakhirah. [1] His saying: leaving one of them, i.e., whichever one. Indeed, adhan and iqamah have been made from the rituals of Islam for congregational mosques, so it is disliked in them to leave either of them. If he enters with his companions in a mosque where prayer has already been performed with adhan and iqamah and prays with the congregation, he does not perform adhan, and there is no harm in iqamah, rather it is better based on the fact that repeating the adhan at one time is confusing, and the iqamah is for those present, and they in the second congregation are not the first ones, so they should perform iqamah. And it is related that Ibn Mas'ud, Uthman, and al-Aswad prayed without adhan and iqamah, Sufyan said: The iqamah of the city sufficed them. See for more traditions in this regard: Musannaf Ibn Abi Shaybah (1:512), Nasb al-Rayah (1:291), al-Dirayah (1:121). And from Ibrahim: Alqamah and al-Aswad entered upon Ibn Mas'ud, he said: Did these pray behind you? We said no. He said: Stand and pray. He said: He did not command us to perform adhan or iqamah. In Musnad Abi Awana (2:165), Sunan al-Bayhaqi al-Kabir (1:406), Musnad al-Shashi (1:416), and its chain is authentic as in I'la al-Sunan (2:125).
Source pages p. 45

Commentary
This passage is a ruling on leaving adhan or iqamah. The original text did not mention the ruling on leaving one of them, so the commentator explains. For the one praying in a mosque in congregation, it is makruh (disliked) to leave one of them because adhan and iqamah are Islamic rituals for congregational mosques. For a traveler, it is permissible to suffice with iqamah only. For a resident praying in his house in a city, it is permissible to leave both because the adhan of the mosque of his quarter suffices, as proven by the saying of Ibn Mas'ud. The commentary also clarifies that if someone enters a mosque where the congregation has already prayed, he should not repeat the adhan but may perform iqamah. The proof from Ibn Mas'ud is cited with several narrations.

Key Terms
أذان (adhan): The call to prayer announcing the beginning of the prayer time, consisting of specified phrases.
إقامة (iqamah): The second call to prayer immediately before the prayer begins, with similar phrases but added 'qad qamat al-salah'.
شعائر الإسلام (sha'air al-Islam): The distinctive rituals and symbols of Islam, outward acts that manifest the faith.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what is the ruling for a traveler regarding the adhan and iqamah?
A) He must perform both.
B) He may omit both.
C) He may suffice with iqamah only.
D) It is disliked to omit either.

Key Takeaway
The adhan and iqamah are communal rituals; omitting them is disliked in the mosque but permissible for a traveler (who retains iqamah) or a resident at home when the mosque's call covers him.

Salah — Prayer
intermediate

When to Stand and Begin Praying During the Adhan and Iqamah
Ruling on when to stand and begin prayer during adhan/iqamah.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on when the imam and congregation should stand and begin the prayer during the adhan and iqamah, and explain the intra-madhhab difference between Abu Hanifah/Muhammad and Abu Yusuf regarding the preferred timing.

Core Idea
The timing of standing and starting prayer is sunnah, not fard, with an intra-Hanafi preference dispute.

This passage from a commentary on al-Hidayah addresses a practical etiquette: when exactly should the imam and congregation stand and begin the prayer during the adhan and iqamah? While the base text gives a clear instruction, the commentary reveals an important intra-Hanafi dispute between the three primary imams about the precise moment. Understanding this difference helps you appreciate how later scholars weighed and resolved conflicting preferred practices, leading to a mufta bihi position that is widely followed today.

Guided Path
1
Identify the Base Ruling
Read the matn: 'The imam and the people stand at ''hayya ''ala al-salah'', and they begin (the prayer) at ''qad qamat al-salah''.' This is the default ruling.
The text does not specify level; the commentary clarifies it is sunnah.
2
Examine the Meaning of ''inda'' (عند)
The commentary explains that the word ''inda'' can mean 'just before' or 'just after'. The ikhtilaf hinges on this linguistic ambiguity.
Both interpretations are valid in Arabic.
3
Recognize the Two Positions
Abu Hanifah and Muhammad hold that 'at' means just before (qabīl), so stand just before 'hayya 'ala al-salah' and start the prayer at 'qad qamat al-salah'. Abu Yusuf holds it means after (ba‘d), so stand after hearing 'hayya' and start after the iqamah is complete.
This is an ikhtilaf in afdaliyyah (preference), not validity.
4
Understand the Rationale
Abu Hanifah and Muhammad emphasize proximity to the announcement. Abu Yusuf prioritizes allowing the muadhdhin to catch the imam's first takbir by delaying the start until after the iqamah.
The reason (ta‘līl) for Abu Yusuf’s view is cited in the commentary.
5
Determine the Mufta Bihi
Many later authorities (al-Haskafi, al-Qari, Ibn Kamal Pasha) prefer Abu Yusuf’s view, as it is the practice in the Two Harams and adopted by the majority (jumhūr).
This does not make the other view invalid; both are acceptable.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

The imam and the people stand at 'hayya 'ala al-salah', and they begin (the prayer) at 'qad qamat al-salah'. And Allah knows best. And the one praying in his house, the adhan of the mosque and its iqamah suffice him, and if there is no mosque in it, then the one praying in his house has the ruling of a traveler. (And the imam and the people stand at 'hayya 'ala al-salah', and they begin at 'qad qamat al-salah'. And Allah knows best.) [1] His saying: And the one praying in his house; it appears that this is an addition (mustadrak) after his saying: 'so the ruling of the one praying in it is as passed.' As indicated in Dhakhirat al-Uqba. And it occurred to me during the composition of al-Si'aya a good explanation: that the pronoun 'in it' in the previous sentence refers to the mosque of the village, not to the village, and the feminine of the pronoun is due to the genitive construction acquiring femininity from the annexed noun. For the annexed noun acquires from the annexing noun matters including femininity, as detailed in Mughni al-Labib and elsewhere. And I discussed it in my treatise Khayr al-Kalam fi Tashih Kalam al-Muluk Muluk al-Kalam. So the previous sentence is an explanation of the ruling for the one praying in the mosques of the villages in congregation, and this sentence is an explanation of the ruling for the one praying in the houses of the villages, so there is no addition (istidrak) or repetition. [2] His saying: The ruling of the traveler; meaning it is disliked for him to leave both and suffice with adhan, and it is permissible for him to suffice with iqamah. [3] His saying: And the imam stands; meaning from their places to the row. And in it there is an indication that when he enters the mosque, it is disliked for him to wait for prayer standing, rather he sits in a place, then stands at 'hayya 'ala al-falah', as explicitly stated in Jami' al-Mudmarat. [4] His saying: 'at' (عند); meaning just before it (the iqamah) according to Abu Hanifah and Muhammad (may Allah have mercy on them), and after it according to Abu Yusuf. And the difference is regarding preference. And the reason is that this (iqamah) is an announcement of the prayer starting, so he should start. (Footnote: Dhakhirah al-Uqba p.77; al-Multaqa chose this saying, al-Halwani said it is correct. As for according to Abu Yusuf, he starts after completing the iqamah. In al-Khulasa: it is more correct, chosen by Ibn Malik in Sharh al-Wiqayah (1/27), and Ibn Kamal Pasha in al-Idah (q13/b), and al-Haskafi said in al-Durr al-Muntaqa (1:78): it is the most just of the schools, said by Ibn al-Sa'ati. And al-Qari said in Fath Bab al-Inaya (1:211): the majority are upon the saying of Abu Yusuf so that the muadhdhin may catch the first of the imam's prayer, and upon it is the practice of the people of the two Harams. And it is mentioned in al-Khazanah: if he does not start until after finishing the iqamah, there is no harm, and the talk is about recommendation (istihbab), not permissibility. See Jami' al-Rumuz (1:79).
Source pages p. 46

Commentary
This passage discusses the proper timing for the imam and congregation to stand during the adhan. The original text states that they stand at 'hayya 'ala al-salah' and begin the prayer at 'qad qamat al-salah'. The commentary explains that 'at' (عند) in 'عند حي على الصلاة' means 'just before it' according to Abu Hanifah and Muhammad, and 'after it' according to Abu Yusuf. This difference is about preference, not validity. Abu Yusuf's view is considered more correct by many later scholars, including the majority (jumhur), so that the muadhdhin can join the first takbir of the imam. The passage also includes a discussion about the ruling for one praying at home, which is considered an addition (mustadrak) but explained as not redundant because it applies to houses in villages, not mosques. The ruling for a traveler is also reiterated: it is disliked to leave both and suffice with adhan, but permissible to suffice with iqamah.

Key Terms
حي على الصلاة (hayya 'ala al-salah): The phrase in the adhan meaning 'come to prayer', at which the congregation stands according to this text.
قد قامت الصلاة (qad qamat al-salah): The phrase in the iqamah meaning 'the prayer has begun', at which the prayer should be started.
عند (inda): In this context, it denotes the time around the utterance of the adhan phrase. It is disputed whether it means just before or just after.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what is the ikhtilaf regarding when the imam should start the prayer?
A) Abu Hanifah and Muhammad say start just before 'qad qamat al-salah', Abu Yusuf says after.
B) All agree to start at 'qad qamat al-salah'.
C) Abu Hanifah and Muhammad say start after iqamah, Abu Yusuf says before.
D) The difference is about the standing, not the start.

Key Takeaway
The timing of standing and starting during adhan/iqamah is sunnah; the Hanafi school has a preference dispute resolved by Abu Yusuf's view, which is widely practiced.

Salah — Prayer

Timing to Begin the Iqamah: The Intra-Hanafi Dispute
Intra-Hanafi dispute on preferred timing of iqamah

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the two Hanafi positions on when the mu'adhdhin should begin the iqamah, explain the dalil for each, and identify the mufta bihi position.

Core Idea
The timing of beginning the iqamah is a matter of preference, not obligation; both views are valid.

This passage delves into a nuanced intra-Hanafi dispute regarding the preferred moment for the mu'adhdhin to commence the iqamah. While the base matn gives a general indication, the commentator expands on the ikhtilaf between Abu Hanifa and Muhammad on one side and Abu Yusuf on the other. Understanding this difference not only clarifies a point of prayer etiquette but also illustrates how later Hanafi scholars weigh and choose between established views.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its ruling level
The act in question is the moment the mu'adhdhin begins the iqamah. The ruling level is sunnah muakkadah (confirmed recommended practice), not wajib. This means both timings are permissible, but one is more virtuous.
The text explicitly states the difference is in preference (أفضلية), not permissibility.
2
State the two positions
Abu Hanifa and Muhammad: begin the iqamah at the phrase 'qad qamati al-salah'. Abu Yusuf: begin after completing the entire iqamah. The commentator clarifies that 'inda (at) means 'just before' for the former and 'just after' for the latter.
This is an intra-Hanafi ikhtilaf; both views are recorded in zahir al-riwayah.
3
Examine the dalil for each position
Abu Hanifa and Muhammad reason that 'qad qamati al-salah' is an announcement that the prayer is starting, so the iqamah should begin at that announcement. Abu Yusuf's reasoning is to allow the mu'adhdhin to catch the beginning of the Imam's prayer, ensuring he can join from the start.
The talil (rationale) for Abu Yusuf is cited by later scholars as more cautious and considerate of the congregation.
4
Identify the thamarah al-khilaf
In practice, if the mu'adhdhin begins at 'qad qamati al-salah', he will be standing and reciting the iqamah while the Imam and people are still rising. If he begins after the iqamah, he will start reciting after everyone has stood, making it easier to join the prayer.
The practical effect is when exactly the mu'adhdhin recites the iqamah relative to the congregation standing.
5
Determine the mufta bihi and reasoning for tarjih
The majority of later Hanafi scholars (e.g., al-Haskafi, Ibn al-Sa'ati, al-Qari) preferred Abu Yusuf's view. They cite the practice of the people of Makkah and Madinah and the benefit that the mu'adhdhin catches the first takbir of the Imam.
This tarjih is based on both textual reasoning and practical benefit (istihsan).

And al-Qari said in 'Fath Bab al-'Inayah' (1:211): the majority are on the saying of Abu Yusuf so that the mu'adhdhin could catch the beginning of the Imam's prayer, and upon it is the practice of the people of the Two Sanctuaries. Source pages p. 46

Commentary
This passage is an intra-Hanafi dispute regarding the preferred time for the mu'adhdhin to begin the iqamah during the adhan. The matn states that the Imam and congregation stand at 'hayya 'ala al-salah' and the mu'adhdhin begins the iqamah at 'qad qamati al-salah'. The commentator first clarifies a previous remark about the one praying in his house, explaining that it is not repetitive. He then addresses the ruling for a person praying alone at home: it is disliked for him to omit both adhan and iqamah, but permissible to omit the adhan and suffice with iqamah. Next, he discusses the proper manner for the Imam: it is disliked to stand waiting for prayer upon entering the mosque; rather, he should sit until the time of 'hayya 'ala al-falah'. The core dispute: 'عند' (at) means just before 'qad qamati al-salah' for Abu Hanifa and Muhammad, while for Abu Yusuf it means after completing the iqamah. The commentator emphasizes that this difference is only about preference (أفضلية), not validity. The reasoning for Abu Hanifa and Muhammad is that the phrase 'qad qamati al-salah' is an announcement that the prayer is commencing, so the mu'adhdhin should begin the iqamah at that moment. Abu Yusuf's reasoning, as preferred by many later scholars including the author of al-Multaqa, al-Halwani, Ibn Malik, Ibn Kamal Pasha, and al-Haskafi, is that beginning after the complete iqamah allows the congregation to catch the beginning of the Imam's prayer. The majority view is that of Abu Yusuf, and it is the practice of the people of Makkah and Madinah. The commentator concludes by noting that if the mu'adhdhin delays until after the iqamah is finished, it is still permissible, as the discussion is about what is recommended.

Key Terms
الإقامة (al-iqamah): The second call to prayer, consisting of specific phrases, indicating that the prayer is about to commence.
عند (‘inda): Here it refers to the temporal proximity: 'just before' or 'just after' depending on the view of the imams.
قَد قَامَتِ الصَّلَاة (qad qamati al-salah): The phrase in the iqamah meaning 'the prayer has been established'.

Key Terms from Source Passage

A) At the phrase 'hayya 'ala al-salah'
B) At the phrase 'qad qamati al-salah'
C) After completing the entire iqamah
D) Before the adhan begins

Key Takeaway
The timing of beginning the iqamah is a recommended practice with two valid positions; the mufta bihi is to begin after completing the iqamah.

Definition of Shart and Types of Impurity in Prayer
Defining the conditions of prayer and differentiating impurity types

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to define the term 'shart' in Hanafi usul and distinguish between hadath and khubth with examples.

Core Idea
A condition is external to the act but necessary for validity; hadath is legal impurity, khubth is physical impurity.

In this lesson, we examine the opening of the chapter on conditions of prayer. The commentator defines what a 'condition' (shart) is in legal theory and then explains the two categories of impurity: legal (hadath) and physical (khubth). We also see an exceptional ruling about praying in a garment that cannot be cleaned. This foundation is essential for understanding the requirements of prayer.

Guided Path
1
Define 'shart' in Hanafi usul
From the text, a shart is external to the essence of the act, and the act's valid existence depends on it. It is neither a cause (sabab) nor a means (mu'aththir). Identify the key phrases: 'kharijan an haqiqatihi' and 'yatawaqqaf alayhi wujuduhu'.
Distinguish shart from rukn and sabab.
2
Distinguish hadath from khubth
Hadath is legal impurity (najasah hukmiyyah) that requires wudu or ghusl. Khubth is physical impurity (najasah haqiqiyyah) like urine or feces. Note that hadath exists only by the Lawgiver's ruling, while khubth is inherently impure.
This dichotomy is characteristic of the Hanafi school.
3
Apply the definition to the garment ruling
The text from al-Zahiriyyah shows an exception: if washing an impure garment will lead to further impurity, it is permissible to pray in it. This is a concession based on necessity.
The general rule is that the garment must be pure, but necessity allows exceptions.

⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

[٣] قوله : وثوبه ؛ قال البرجندي : ينبغي أن يعم الثوبُ بحيث يشمل القلنسوة والنعل والحف، ونحوها في «الظهيرية»: «إذا صلّى في ثوب نجس ويعلمُ أنَّه لو غسلَهُ تنجس ثانياً وثالثاً جاز أن يصلي فيه». [٤] قوله : ومكانه ؛ أي بقدر موضع قدميه وموضع سجوده، فلو كان الخبث في موضع ركبتيه أو يديه لم يمنع ؛ لأن اتصالها ليس بفرض () ، قاله البرجندي. (۱) وتفصيل المسألة حتى لا يقع إيهام ، قال ابن الهمام في فتح القدير»(۱ : ۱۹۱): «والمعتبر في طهارة المكان موضع القدم رواية واحدة، وموضع السجود في أصح الروايتين عن أبي حنيفة وهو قولهما : ولا يجب طهارة موضع الركبتين واليدين ؛ لأن وضعها ليس فرضاً عندهم. لكن في فتاوى قاضي خان وكذا لو كانت النجاسة في موضع السجود أو موضع الركبتين أو اليدين : يعني تجمع وتمنع فإنه قدم هذين اللفظين حكما لما إذا كانت النجاسة تحت كل قدم أقل من درهم، ولو جمعت صارت أكثر من درهم قال ولا يجعل كأنه لم يضع العضو على النجاسة، وهذا كما لو صلى رافعاً إحدى قدميه جازت صلاته ، ولو وضع القدم على النجاسة . لا يجوز ولا يجعل كأنه لم يضع. انتهى لفظه. وهو يفيد أن عدم اشتراط طهارة مكان اليدين والركبتين هو إذا لم يضعهما، أما إن وضعهما اشترطت فليحفظ هذا.
Chapter of the Conditions of Prayer: (They are the purification of the body of the worshipper from hadath and khubth, and his garment, and his place.) [1] His saying: 'conditions': a condition of a thing is that which is external to its essence and upon which its existence depends, without being a means to it or an effective cause. If it is effective, it is a cause; if it is a means, it is a reason. [2] His saying: 'hadath': the legal impurity (al-najasah al-hukmiyyah): i.e., that which the Lawgiver has ruled as impurity, and it is established that He has made it like the impurity of the junub and the hadath. And the real impurity (al-haqiqiyyah) is that which is truly impurity without needing the Lawgiver's ruling, such as excrement, urine, and the like. [3] His saying: 'and his garment': al-Birjandi said: it is appropriate that 'garment' be general so as to include the cap, the sandal, the shoe, and similar items. In 'al-Zahiriyyah': 'If he prays in a garment that is impure, knowing that if he washes it, it will become impure a second and third time, it is permissible for him to pray in it.' [4] His saying: 'and his place': i.e., to the extent of the place of his feet and the place of his prostration. And if the impurity is at the place of his knees or hands, it does not prevent (validity), because their contact (with the ground) is not obligatory. (1) And the detail of the issue so that no misunderstanding occurs: Ibn al-Humam said in 'Fath al-Qadir' (1:191): 'What is considered in the purity of the place is the place of the foot according to one narration, and the place of prostration according to the most authentic of the two narrations from Abu Hanifa, and it is the saying of the two (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad): It is not obligatory to purify the place of the knees and hands, because placing them is not obligatory according to them. However, in the 'Fatawa of Qadi Khan', and likewise, if the impurity is at the place of prostration or the place of the knees or hands: meaning they accumulate and prevent, then he presented these two wordings as a legal ruling for when the impurity under each foot is less than a dirham, and if they are collected, it becomes more than a dirham. He said: And it is not considered as if he did not place the limb on the impurity. This is like if he prays lifting one foot, his prayer is valid, but if he places the foot on the impurity, it is not valid, and it is not considered as if he did not place it.' End of his quote. And it indicates that not requiring purity of the place of the hands and knees is when he does not place them, but if he places them, then it is required. So remember this.
Source pages p. 47

Commentary
This passage is the opening of the chapter on the conditions (shurut) of prayer in the commentary 'Umdat al-Ri'ayah. It begins by stating that the conditions of prayer are three: purification of the worshipper's body from both hadath (ritual impurity) and khubth (physical impurity), purification of his garment, and purification of his place. The commentator then defines a 'condition' (shart) as something external to the essence of the act but upon which its valid existence depends. It is neither a cause nor a means but a prerequisite. Next, he explains the two types of impurity: hadath refers to legal (hukmi) impurity, which requires wudu or ghusl and only becomes impure through the ruling of the Lawgiver; khubth refers to real (haqiqi) impurity, which is self-evidently impure like excrement or urine. For the garment, he cites al-Birjandi that 'thawb' (garment) includes cap, sandal, shoe, etc. It also cites a ruling from al-Zahiriyyah: if a person prays in a garment that is impure but knows that washing it would make it impure again (e.g., because the water is contaminated), it is permissible to pray in it due to necessity. Regarding the place, the requirement is limited to where the feet are placed and where the forehead goes in prostration; impurity on the knees or hands does not invalidate the prayer because placing these limbs on the ground is not obligatory (fard) according to the Hanafi school. This is the view of Abu Yusuf and Muhammad, and it is the preferred one. However, the commentator adds a crucial detail from Ibn al-Humam and Qadi Khan: if the worshipper actually places his hands or knees on a spot with impurity, then the prayer is not valid, because he has brought his limb into contact with impurity. Similarly, if the impurity under each foot is less than a dirham combined, it is overlooked, but if they add up to more, it is problematic.

Key Terms
حدث (hadath): Ritual impurity that necessitates wudu or ghusl; it is a legal impurity (najasah hukmiyyah) that exists only by virtue of the Lawgiver's ruling.
خبث (khubth): Physical impurity (najasah haqiqiyyah) such as urine, feces, blood, etc., that is inherently unclean and must be removed from the body, garments, and place of prayer.
شرط (shart): A condition: something external to the essence of an act (here prayer) upon which the validity of the act depends. It is not itself part of the act's essence (rukn) nor a cause (sabab).

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what is the difference between hadath and khubth?
A) Hadath is physical impurity, khubth is legal impurity.
B) Hadath is legal impurity that requires wudu/ghusl, khubth is physical impurity like urine.
C) Both are the same but hadath is for the body and khubth for the garment.
D) Hadath is a condition, khubth is a pillar.

Key Takeaway
The conditions of prayer are purification from hadath and khubth on body, garment, and place; a shart is an external prerequisite, not an integral part of the act.

Salah — Prayer

Purity of Place in Prayer: The Hanafi Position and Intra-Madhhab Detail
Detailing the requirement of purity of place and the intra-Hanafi debate

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain the Hanafi requirement for purity of the place of prayer, including the differing views on hands and knees, and apply the ruling when limbs are placed on impurity.

Core Idea
Only the place of feet and prostration must be pure; hands and knees are required only if placed on impurity.

This lesson focuses on the fourth condition: purity of the place. The commentator elaborates on what area must be pure, and we find an important intra-Hanafi dispute. The relied-upon view is that only the spots where the feet and forehead touch are required to be pure, but if one actually places hands or knees on impurity, the prayer is affected. This detail is crucial for practical application.

Guided Path
1
Identify the baseline ruling
The text states that the place must be pure 'to the extent of the place of his feet and the place of his prostration'. Impurity on the knees or hands does not prevent validity because their contact is not fard.
This is the default Hanafi position from the Sahibayn.
2
Note the intra-Hanafi difference
Ibn al-Humam reports that the place of the foot is required by one narration from Abu Hanifa, while the place of prostration is required by the most authentic narration, which is also the view of Abu Yusuf and Muhammad. They agree that hands and knees are not required.
The Sahibayn require both feet and forehead spots to be pure.
3
Understand the qualification from Qadi Khan
If the impurity under each foot is less than a dirham but adds up to more, it is problematic. Also, if one places a limb (like hand or knee) on impurity, that makes the prayer invalid, unlike the default where not placing them does not require purity.
This is a critical practical detail: the rule changes when actual contact occurs.

Source Text

Key Terms
موضع القدمين (mawdi' al-qadamayn): The place where the feet rest during prayer; must be pure.
موضع السجود (mawdi' al-sujud): The place where the forehead rests during prostration; must be pure.
الرّكبتين واليدين (al-rukbatayn wa al-yadayn): The knees and hands; their placement spot is not required to be pure unless actually placed.

Conditions of Prayer (Shurut al-Salah)
Defining the six preconditions for valid prayer

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the six conditions (shurut) for the validity of prayer according to the Hanafi school and apply them to scenarios involving partial impurity or uncovering.

Core Idea
Six preconditions must be met before prayer: purity, covering, facing Qibla, and intention.

This passage lists and explains the six essential conditions that must be present for prayer to be valid in the Hanafi school. Understanding these conditions is critical because missing any one renders the prayer invalid. The text also clarifies nuances such as the difference between hadath and khabath, and that seeing one's own 'awrah does not invalidate prayer.

Guided Path
1
Identify the six conditions
From the text, list the conditions: purity of body, garment, and place; covering 'awrah; facing Qibla; intention.
The matn states them succinctly; the commentary expands.
2
Distinguish hadath and khabath
Define hadath (ritual impurity requiring wudu/ghusl) and khabath (physical impurity requiring washing). Both must be removed from body, garment, and place.
This distinction is unique to Hanafi fiqh.
3
Analyze the place purity requirement
Note the intra-Hanafi ikhtilaf: purity is mandatory for the place of feet and prostration, but not for knees and hands unless they touch impurity.
The mufta bihi view follows Abu Hanifa's most authentic narration.
4
Understand covering 'awrah
The obligation is relative to others; if one sees his own 'awrah due to a torn garment, the prayer remains valid.
The proof is the command 'Take your adornment at every mosque' (7:31).
5
Recognize the evidence
The commentator cites Quranic verses: 7:31 for covering and 2:144 for facing Qibla. Both are commands indicating obligation.
The dalal is zanni; the commands are interpreted contextually.

الأول : يشترط للمكي مطلقاً حتى لو صلى في بيته يجب أن يصلي بحيث لو أزيل الجدران وقع الاستقبال على عين الكعبة، وهو ظاهر كلام صاحب المبسوط»(۱۰ : ۱۹۰)، و«التبيين» (۱ : ۱۰۱)، و((الهداية» (۱) : (۲۷۰) ، و درر الحكام (۱) : (٦١)، و«هدية الصعلوك» (ص٥٢)، ومنحة السلوك» (١ : ١٦٨). والقول الثاني : الفرض للمكي المشاهد للكعبة إصابة عينها ؛ لقدرته عليه يقيناً، وفرض غير المشاهد البعيد والقريب إصابة جهة الكعبة وهو اختيار صاحب «الدراية» وصاحب «الهداية» في «التجنيس» كما في حاشية الشلبي (۱) : (۱۰۰)، ومشى عليه صاحب «مراقي الفلاح (ص۲۱۲)، قال الشرنبلالي في حاشيته على الدرر (۱): ٦٠): «لا إجماع على اشتراط عينها في حق غير المكي بل الأصح أنه كالغائب للزوم الحرج في إلزام حقيقة المسامتة في كل بقعة يصلي فيها كما في «الفتح» و«البرهان» ». (٤) (السعاية» (٢ : ٦٥ - ٦٦).
Chapter of Conditions of Prayer: They are: purity of the body of the worshiper from hadath and khabath, his garment, and his place; covering his nakedness; facing the Qibla; and intention.
Source pages p. 47

Commentary
This passage is a ruling statement listing the six conditions (shurut) for the validity of prayer according to the Hanafi school. The author, in the commentary, expands on each condition. First, he defines 'shart' (condition) as something external to the essence of the act but necessary for its existence, distinguishing it from cause ('illa) and means (sabab). For purity, 'hadath' is ritual impurity (a legal impurity), while 'khabath' is actual impurity (e.g., urine, feces). The place of prayer must be pure at least where the feet and forehead (for prostration) are placed; the pureness of where knees and hands is not obligatory unless they touch the impurity, as per the relied-upon view. For covering the 'awrah, the obligation is only so that others do not see it; if one sees his own 'awrah due to an open collar, the prayer is not invalidated. The proof is the Quranic verse 'Take your adornment at every mosque' (7:31). For facing the Qibla, it means facing the direction of the Ka'bah; the verse 'turn your face toward the Sacred Mosque' (2:144) is evidence. There is a difference of opinion whether the Meccan must face the exact 'ayn of the Ka'bah or only its direction, but the preferred view is that only those who see the Ka'bah must face its exact 'ayn; others may face its direction. Finally, intention (niyyah) means intending the worship for Allah alone.

Key Terms
الحدث (al-hadath): Ritual (legal) impurity that requires ablution or bathing.
الخبث (al-khabath): Actual physical impurity (e.g., urine, feces) that is removed by washing.
العورة (al-‘awrah): The body parts that must be covered during prayer: for men, from navel to knees; for women, the whole body except face, hands, and feet.
النية (al-niyyah): Intention in the heart to perform the specific prayer for Allah.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi school, what is the ruling if a person prays with an open collar and accidentally sees his own chest (which is part of the 'awrah for men)?
A) The prayer is invalid and must be repeated.
B) The prayer is valid because the obligation of covering 'awrah is so that others do not see it.
C) The prayer is valid but requires sajdah al-sahw.
D) The prayer is makruh but valid.

Key Takeaway
The six conditions for valid prayer are purity from hadath and khabath, covering 'awrah, facing Qibla, and intention; missing any invalidates the prayer.

Three Conditions of Prayer: Covering 'Awrah, Facing Qibla, Intention
Listing and proving three essential conditions for prayer

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the three conditions of prayer and identify the Quranic proof for each.

Core Idea
Covering 'awrah, facing qibla, and intention are conditions for prayer; omission invalidates.

In this passage from 'Umdat al-Ri'ayah, the author lists three essential conditions for the validity of prayer: covering the private parts, facing the qibla, and forming the intention. Each is supported by a clear Quranic proof. The commentary also clarifies that covering 'awrah is obligatory even if one is alone, and that facing the exact Kaaba is only required for Meccans, with a difference of opinion.

Guided Path
1
Identify the three conditions
Name the three conditions listed in the passage exactly as stated.
They are covering 'awrah, facing qibla, and intention.
2
Examine the proof for covering 'awrah
Read the commentary: Surah al-A'raf 31 is cited, interpreting 'adornment' as clothing, implying covering private parts.
The imperative 'take your adornment' is taken as a command.
3
Examine the proof for facing qibla
Surah al-Baqarah 144 commands turning toward al-Masjid al-Haram. The commentary notes that exact Kaaba is not required except for the Meccan, with a difference of opinion.
This shows the Hanafi approach to interpreting direction.
4
Understand the role of intention
Intention is defined as the intention of worship for Allah. It is a condition of the heart.
No specific verse is quoted for intention in this passage.
5
Note the intra-Hanafi difference on facing the exact Kaaba
Some Hanafi scholars require exact alignment for Meccans; others consider direction sufficient. The text states it is not required except for the Meccan, with a difference.
This exemplifies how ikhtilaf exists even within a school.

Definition of 'Awrah for Men, Slave Women, and Free Women with Intra-Hanafi Dispute
Defining 'awrah for different categories with intra-Hanafi disputes.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi definition of 'awrah for men, slave women, and free women, and identify the relied-upon position regarding the foot and hand in prayer.

Core Idea
The 'awrah differs by status: for men navel to knee, for slave women plus back and belly, for free women all but face, hands, and feet — with internal disagreement on the foot.

This passage from 'Umdat al-Ri'ayah defines the 'awrah (parts to be covered) in prayer for three categories: men, female slaves, and free women. It also reveals an intra-Hanafi dispute about whether the foot and the outer hand are considered 'awrah for free women. Understanding these definitions is essential for valid prayer.

Guided Path
1
Identify the three categories and their definitions
Men: navel to knee. Slave women: same plus back and belly. Free women: all except face, hands, feet.
The passage gives the concise definitions in the first sentence.
2
Understand the scope of 'female slave'
It includes all types: regular slave, mudabbarah, mukatabah.
The commentary explicitly mentions the inclusive scope.
3
Recognize the dispute on the hand
The relied-upon (mu'tamad) position is that the outer hand is not 'awrah, as per Fatāwā Qāḍī Khān and Ḥalbat al-Mujallā.
This is a clear tarjih within the school.
4
Recognize the three opinions on the foot
(1) Not 'awrah (corrected in al-Hidāyah), (2) 'awrah (al-Aqṭaʿ), (3) 'awrah only in prayer.
The passage lists all three with authorities.
5
Determine the mufta bihi
The operative position: foot and outer hand are not 'awrah, following al-Hidāyah and the majority of later scholars.
This is the ruling for fatwa today.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
والعورة للرجل من تحت سرته إلى تحت ركبته وللأمة مثلُه مع ظهرها وبطنها ، وللحرّة كل بدنها إلا الوجه والكفّ والقدم. [4] قوله : والعورة... الخ ؛ لما فرغ عن ذكر الشروط الستة وكان قد فرغ من تفصيل الثلاثة الأول في كتاب الطهارات أراد أن يبين تفصيلاً متعلقا بالثلاثة الباقية. [2] قوله : وللأمة ؛ سواءً كانت رقيقة أو مدبرة أو مكاتبة. [3] قوله : والكف ؛ وكذا ظاهرُ الكفّ ليس بعورة على المعتمد، كما في «فتاوى قاضي خان» و«حَلْبة المجلّى». [4] قوله : والقدم؛ هذا أحد الأقوال الثلاثة، وصححه في «الهداية». وثانيهما : إنّه عورة، وصححه الأقطع شارح مختصر القدوري. وثالثهما : إنّه عورة في الصلاة فيجب ستره لا خارجها.
And the 'awrah for the man is from below his navel to below his knee, and for the female slave it is similar to his with her back and belly, and for the free woman it is all her body except the face, hands, and feet. [4] His statement: 'And the 'awrah...' etc.; when he finished mentioning the six conditions and had finished detailing the first three in the Book of Purification, he wanted to give a detail related to the remaining three. [2] His statement: 'And for the female slave'; whether she is a regular slave, a mudabbarah, or a mukatabah. [3] His statement: 'And the hand'; likewise the outer hand is not 'awrah according to the relied-upon position, as in 'Fatāwā Qāḍī Khān' and 'Ḥalbat al-Mujallā'. [4] His statement: 'And the foot'; this is one of three opinions, and it was considered correct in 'al-Hidāyah'. The second: it is 'awrah, and was considered correct by al-Aqṭaʿ, the commentator of Mukhtaṣar al-Qudūrī. And the third: it is 'awrah in prayer so it must be covered, not outside of it.
Source pages p. 49

Commentary
This passage defines the 'awrah (parts to be covered) in prayer for different categories. For men: from navel to knee. For female slaves: same as men plus back and belly. For free women: entire body except face, hands, and feet. The commentary then expands: it explains that 'female slave' includes all types of slaves. Regarding the hand, the relied-upon (mu'tamad) position is that the outer hand is not 'awrah. For the foot, there are three opinions within the Hanafi school: (1) it is not 'awrah (corrected in al-Hidayah), (2) it is 'awrah (al-Aqta'), (3) it is 'awrah only inside prayer but not outside. The passage thus highlights an intra-Hanafi ikhtilaf. The practical consequence is whether exposing the foot invalidates prayer. The mufta bihi (operative position) follows al-Hidayah: the foot is not 'awrah.

Key Terms
العورة (al-'awrah): The parts of the body that must be covered in prayer, defined differently for men, slave women, and free women.
الأمة (al-ummah): A female slave, including mudabbarah (one promised freedom upon master's death) and mukatabah (one who has a contract of manumission).
الحرة (al-ḥurrah): A free woman.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi school defines 'awrah based on status: men navel to knee, slave women plus back/belly, free women all but face, hands, and feet; the foot and outer hand are not 'awrah per the mufta bihi, despite intra-school disagreement.

The 'Awrah in Prayer: Men, Slave Women, and Free Women
Defining 'awrah with intra-Hanafi detail on the foot

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the defined 'awrah for men, slave women, and free women according to the Hanafi school and identify the three opinions on the foot of a free woman.

Core Idea
The 'awrah differs by status: men navel-knee, slave women same plus back/belly, free women all except face, hands, feet.

Covering the 'awrah is a condition for the validity of prayer. The Hanafi school defines it precisely for each category. This passage from 'Umdat al-Ri'aya explains the basic ruling and then zooms in on two points of detail: the outer hand and the foot of a free woman. The foot, in particular, has three transmitted opinions within the school, and knowing which is relied upon is essential for practice.

Guided Path
1
Identify the three categories and their 'awrah
The matn gives a baseline: for men, from below the navel to below the knee; for slave women, the same plus the back and belly; for free women, the entire body except the face, hands, and feet. Note that the exception for the free woman includes the foot.
The slave woman's 'awrah is similar to a man's but with added back and belly.
2
Understand the scope of 'slave woman'
The commentary clarifies that 'slave woman' (al-ummah) includes a female slave who is fully owned (raqīq), a mudabbara (whose freedom is deferred), and a mukātaba (who has a contract of manumission). All share the same 'awrah.
3
Examine the two details: outer hand and foot
The commentary states that the reliable view (al-mu‘tamad) in Fatawa Qadi Khan and Halba al-Mujalla is that the outer hand (zāhir al-kaff) is not 'awrah. For the foot, three opinions are recorded.
4
Analyze the three opinions on the foot
(1) The foot is not 'awrah at all – the view preferred in al-Hidayah by al-Marghinani. (2) The foot is 'awrah – the view preferred by al-Aqta‘, a commentator on Mukhtasar al-Quduri. (3) The foot is 'awrah only during prayer (must be covered inside prayer, not outside).
The third opinion attempts a middle ground.
5
Determine the mufta bihi (operative ruling)
The relied-upon position (mufta bihi) is the first opinion: the foot is not 'awrah. This is based on the tarjīh of al-Marghinani in al-Hidayah and is the standard for fatwa.
Intra-Hanafi ikhtilaf is resolved by authority of the later masters.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

[1] قوله : والعورة... الخ ؛ لما فرغ عن ذكر الشروط الستة وكان قد فرغ من تفصيل الثلاثة الأول في كتاب الطهارات أراد أن يبين تفصيلاً متعلقا بالثلاثة الباقية. Commentary
This passage defines the ‘awrah (must-cover parts) for prayer. For men: navel to knee. For slave women: same plus back and belly. For free women: all except face, hands, feet. Commentary clarifies slaves include owned, mudabbara, mukataba. The reliable view is that the outer hand is not ‘awrah (per Fatawa Qadi Khan). For the foot, three opinions exist: (1) not ‘awrah (al-Hidayah), (2) ‘awrah (al-Aqta‘), (3) ‘awrah only in prayer (must cover). The discussion shows intra-Hanafi ikhtilaf.

Key Terms
عورة (‘awrah): Body parts that must be covered during prayer and before non-mahram.
الأمة (al-ummah): A female slave, including mudabbara and mukataba.

Additional ‘Awrah Rulings: Hair, Quarter of Male Organ, Testicles
Clarifying that descending hair, quarter of male organ, and testicles are ‘awrah

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify which body parts are considered separate ‘awrah members according to the Hanafi school, and state the ruling when a quarter of any such member is exposed during prayer.

Core Idea
Exposure of a quarter of any separate ‘awrah member, including descending hair and testicles, invalidates prayer.

This passage from ‘Umdat al-Ri‘āya clarifies that not only the main ‘awrah areas but also additional parts like descending hair and testicles are considered separate members. The Hanafi rule that exposure of a quarter of any such member nullifies the prayer reinforces the precision required in covering during salah.

Guided Path
1
Identify the ruling
Determine which parts are explicitly mentioned as ‘awrah in the passage: descending hair, quarter of the male organ, and testicles.
These are additions to the standard ‘awrah.
2
Distinguish head hair from descending hair
Recognize that hair on the head is uncontested ‘awrah, but hanging hair is a separate member and its status is disputed.
The correct view is that it is ‘awrah.
3
Apply the quarter rule
Understand that each separate member has its own quarter threshold. Exposure of a quarter or more of any such member invalidates the prayer.
Less than a quarter is not invalidating.
4
Note the dispute and mufta bihi
Some scholars say hanging hair is not ‘awrah; the relied-upon (mufta bihi) position is that it is ‘awrah.
The commentator affirms this with 'as-sahih'.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

قوله : نزل من رأسها ؛ قيد به لأنه لا خلاف في الشعر الذي على الرأس في أنه عورة إنما الخلافُ في المسترسل فقيل : ليس بعورة، والصحيح أنه عورة.

And hair that descends from her head, and a quarter of his male organ individually, and the testicles prevent. He said: 'Descends from her head' is a condition, because there is no dispute about hair on the head being ‘awrah; the dispute is about hanging hair, it is said it is not ‘awrah, but the correct view is that it is ‘awrah. The conclusion is that a quarter of a member that is ‘awrah prevents the validity of prayer. So the head is a member, the descending hair is another member, the male organ is a member, and the testicles are another.
Source pages p. 50

Commentary
The passage adds details: hair descending from a woman’s head, a quarter of a man’s penis, and the testicles are each ‘awrah. The commentator notes that hair on the head is uncontested, but loose hanging hair is disputed; the correct view is it is ‘awrah. The principle: uncovering a quarter of any individual ‘awrah member invalidates the prayer, since each is a separate member.

Key Terms
شعر (sha‘r): Hair; specifically, hair that descends from the head is a separate ‘awrah member.
ربع (rub‘): A quarter; exposure of a quarter of an ‘awrah member invalidates prayer.

Praying with Impurity When Unable to Remove It
Ruling that one unable to remove impurity prays with it without repeating

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling for one who cannot remove impurity and explain the legal cause ('illa) for not repeating the prayer.

Core Idea
Unable to remove impurity? Pray anyway and never repeat — obligation falls with inability.

This lesson addresses a critical concession in Hanafi fiqh: what if you have impurity on your body or clothes but lack any means to remove it? The matn rules that you pray in that state and do not repeat the prayer. The commentator explains the reason: the obligation to purify is lifted due to inability ('ajz). Understanding this principle of rukhsa (concession) is essential for grasping how Islamic law accommodates human limitation.

Guided Path
1
Identify the person and the act
The ruling concerns a person who has no means to remove impurity. They are obligated to pray despite the impurity.
2
Extract the ruling parts
The ruling has two parts: (1) they pray with the impurity, (2) they do not repeat the prayer.
3
Locate the 'illa (legal cause)
The commentator gives the 'illa: 'because the obligation of purification falls from him due to inability.' This is the proof.
4
Understand the underlying principle
The 'illa is 'ajz (inability). In Hanafi usul, inability lifts obligations (al-mashaqqa tajlib al-taysir).
5
Apply to similar cases
This principle applies elsewhere, e.g., one who cannot find water performs tayammum and does not repeat.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وعَادِمُ مُزيلِ النَّجِسِ صلى معه، ولم يعد
قوله : ولم يعد ; لأنَّ تكليف التطهير سقط عنه للعجز.
And one who lacks a means to remove impurity prays with it and does not repeat. He said: 'And does not repeat' because the obligation of purification falls from him due to inability.
Source pages p. 50

Commentary
This is a ruling for those who cannot remove impurity (e.g., no water or means). They pray with the impurity and do not repeat the prayer later. The commentator gives the ‘illa: the obligation to purify is dropped due to inability (‘ajz). This is a principle of rukhsa (concession).

Key Terms
عادم مزيل النجس (‘ādim muzīl al-najis): One who lacks any means to remove impurity (water, etc.).

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
Why does a person who cannot remove impurity not repeat the prayer?
A) Because the impurity is excused altogether
B) Because the obligation of purification is dropped due to inability
C) Because the prayer becomes optional
D) Because the impurity is considered pure in that state

Key Takeaway
One unable to remove impurity prays with it and does not repeat because the obligation to purify is lifted due to inability.

Praying Naked with a Clean Quarter of Garment
Ruling on praying with an impure garment when a clean quarter is available or not.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on praying with an impure garment when exactly a quarter is clean versus when less is clean, and explain the rationale of preferring covering over nakedness as the lesser evil.

Core Idea
When forced between impurity and nakedness, covering is the lesser evil and thus preferred.

This lesson examines the situation where a person has an impure garment but a part of it is clean. The ruling hinges on whether the clean portion is at least a quarter of the garment. The underlying principle is that covering the 'awrah is more important than absolute purity of clothing, and when both cannot be achieved, the lesser evil is chosen.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two scenarios
The passage discusses two possibilities: the garment has at least a quarter of its area pure, or less than a quarter is pure.
2
State the ruling for each scenario
If a quarter is pure, praying naked is not permissible; one must cover that quarter. If less than a quarter is pure, one has a choice between praying naked or wearing the impure garment, but covering is preferred.
3
Understand the rationale
Each option (nakedness, impurity) normally prevents valid prayer. Since the amounts (quarter vs less) are equal in effect, they are equivalent. However, covering is less severe because wearing impure clothing is permissible outside prayer while nakedness is not.
4
Apply the principle of choosing the lesser evil
The commentator compares the situation to one afflicted with two afflictions, where the lesser is chosen. This is a rational proof based on necessity and severity.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فإن صلى عاريا وربع ثوبِهِ طاهر لم يجز، وفي أقل من ربع الأفضلُ صلاتُهُ فيه
قوله : الأفضل ; يعني هو مخير بين أن يصلّي عُرياناً وبين أن يُصلِّي في ذلك النجس ; لأنَّ كلاً منهما مانع عن جواز الصلاة حالة الاختيار، ومستو في حق المقدار فتساويا، والأفضل هو التستّر، فإن لبس الثوب النجس أهون من كونه عُريانا، حتى جاز الأول خارج الصلاة لا الثاني، ومن ابتلي ببليتين يختار أهونهما.
Then if he prays naked while a quarter of his garment is pure, it is not permissible. And in less than a quarter, the preferred option is to pray in it. He said: 'The preferred' means he is given a choice between praying naked and praying in that impure (garment), because each is a preventer of prayer validity under normal circumstances, and they are equal in quantity, so they are equivalent. But the preferred is to cover, because wearing an impure garment is less severe than being naked, such that the former is allowed outside prayer while the latter is not. And one who is afflicted with two afflictions chooses the lesser of them.
Source pages p. 50

Commentary
This passage gives the ruling for someone who has a garment but it is impure. If he has a pure quarter of a garment, he cannot pray naked; he must cover that quarter. If less than a quarter, he has a choice: pray naked or cover with the impurity. Covering is preferred because it is less severe than nakedness. The commentator applies the principle that when faced with two evils, one chooses the lesser.

Key Terms
تستّر (tasattur): Covering oneself; prioritized over purity of garment.
الربع (al-rubu'): One quarter; the threshold for mandatory covering in this context.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
If a person has a garment that is less than a quarter of his body and it is impure, what is the preferred action?
A) Pray naked
B) Wear the impure garment
C) Wait for a clean garment
D) Postpone prayer

Key Takeaway
When forced, covering the 'awrah with an impure garment is better than praying naked, as covering is the lesser evil.

Covering the 'Awrah: Definition and Exposure Thresholds
Defining 'awrah and exposure limits

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to define the 'awrah in Hanafi fiqh and state the threshold of exposure that invalidates prayer.

Core Idea
Exposing a quarter of a limb invalidates prayer; hair that falls from the head is part of the 'awrah.

The validity of prayer depends on covering the 'awrah. Different body parts have different rulings, and the Hanafi school uses a precise measure – a quarter of a limb – to determine when exposure breaks the prayer.

Guided Path
1
Identify the 'awrah for men and women
Recall that for men, the 'awrah is from navel to knees (including knees). For women, it is the entire body except face, hands, and feet.
The text specifically addresses hair that has fallen from a woman's head, which is considered a separate limb.
2
Understand the quarter threshold
Exposure of a quarter of a limb (e.g., a quarter of the male organ) invalidates prayer. Less than a quarter does not, though covering is recommended.
The quarter is a standard Hanafi measure; other schools may use different benchmarks.
3
Analyze the dispute about hanging hair
The text notes disagreement about whether hair hanging down from the head (not attached) is 'awrah. The correct Hanafi view is that it is 'awrah.
This dispute affects rulings on looking at a non-mahram woman's hair.
4
Apply the ruling to testicles
The two testicles are considered a separate limb; their full exposure prevents the validity of prayer.
The text says 'the two testicles prevent' meaning they are 'awrah and must be covered.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وشعرٍ نزل من رأسها، وربع ذكره منفرداً، والأُنثَيين يمنع، وعادِمُ مزيل النَّجس صلى معه، ولم يعد، فإن صلى عارياً وربع ثوبه طاهر لم يجز، وفي أقل من ربع الأفضل صلاته فيه، ومن عَدمَ ثوباً فصلى قائماً جاز، وقاعداً مومئاً ندب.

قوله: نزل من رأسها؛ قيد به لأنه لا خلاف في الشعر الذي على الرأس في أنه عورة إنما الخلاف في المسترسل فقيل: ليس بعورة، والصحيح أنه عورة. قوله: ولم يعد؛ لأن تكليف التطهير سقط عنه للعجز. قوله: الأفضل؛ يعني هو مخير بين أن يصلي عُرياناً وبين أن يُصلي في ذلك النجس؛ لأن كلاً منهما مانع عن جواز الصلاة حالة الاختيار، ومستو في حق المقدار فتساويا، والأفضل هو التستّر، فإن لبس الثوب النجس أهون من كونه عُرياناً، حتى جاز الأول خارج الصلاة لا الثاني، ومن ابتلي ببليتين يختار أهونهما. قوله: نُدِبَ؛ إما مصدر بمعنى المفعول، أو ماض مجهول، يعني: إنه مخير بين أن يصلي قائماً بركوع وسجود، وبين أن يصلي قاعداً بالإيماء؛ لأن في القيام أداء الأركان، وفي القعود ستر في الجملة، فيميل إلى أيهما شاء، والأفضل هو الثاني؛ لأن الأركان لهما خلف وهو الإيماء، ولا خلف الستر العورة.

الأنثيان: الخصيتان. ينظر: «الصحاح»(١ : ٥١). أي الخبث عدماً حقيقياً أو حكمياً، كما إذا كان معه ماء، لكن يخاف العطش، فإن صلى مع النجس للضرورة لم يُعد، وإن كان الوقت باقياً؛ لأنه فعل ما في وسعه. كذا في «فتح باب العناية» (١ : ٢٢٠). وفي «المحيط»: الأصح أنه عورة وإلا جاز النظر إلى صدغ الأجنبية وطرف ناصيتها وهو يؤدي إلى الفتنة. وفي الغياثية: ولا بأس بالنظر إلى شعر الكافرة. ينظر: «التبيين» (٦ : ١٧)، «درر الحكام» (١ : ٥٩)، «الشرنبلالية» (١ : ٥٩)، وغيرها.
And hair that has fallen from her head, and a quarter of his organ alone, and the two testicles prevent. And one who lacks a means of removing impurity prays with it and does not repeat. For if he prays naked while a quarter of his garment is pure, it is not permissible. And in less than a quarter, it is better for him to pray in it. And one who lacks a garment, if he prays standing, it is permissible; and sitting gesturing is recommended.

His statement: 'hair that has fallen from her head' – he restricted it because there is no disagreement about the hair on the head being 'awrah; the disagreement is about the hanging hair, so it was said it is not 'awrah, and the correct view is that it is 'awrah. His statement: 'does not repeat' – because the obligation of purification has fallen from him due to inability. His statement: 'the best' – meaning he has a choice between praying naked and praying in that impurity, because each of them is a preventer of the permissibility of prayer in the state of ability, and they are equal in terms of quantity, so they are equal; but the best is to cover, because wearing the impure garment is lighter than being naked, so much so that the first is allowed outside the prayer while the second is not, and one who is afflicted with two afflictions chooses the lighter of them. His statement: 'recommended' – either a verbal noun in the sense of the passive, or a passive past verb; meaning he has a choice between praying standing with bowing and prostration, and praying sitting with gestures, because in standing there is performance of the pillars, and in sitting there is covering in general, so he may incline to whichever he wishes, but the better is the second, because the pillars have a substitute for them, which is gesture, and covering the 'awrah has no substitute.

The testicles: meaning the two testes. See: al-Sihah (1:51). That is, impurity, whether actual or legal, as when he has water but fears thirst; then if he prays with the impurity out of necessity, he does not repeat, even if the time remains, because he did what he could. Thus in Fath Bab al-Inayah (1:220). And in al-Muhit: the soundest view is that it is 'awrah, otherwise it would be permissible to look at the temple and the tip of the forelock of a foreign woman, which leads to fitna. And in al-Ghiyathiyyah: There is no harm in looking at the hair of a disbelieving woman. See: al-Tabyin (6:17), Durar al-Hukkam (1:59), al-Shurnubulaliyyah (1:59), etc.
Source pages p. 50

Commentary
This passage from the commentary provides detailed rulings on the conditions of covering the 'awrah (intimate parts) and removing impurity during prayer. The rulings are from the Hanafi school. The text begins by listing what constitutes a valid covering: hair that has fallen from a woman's head (i.e., not attached to the scalp) is considered a separate limb. For a man, a quarter of his organ alone (without the testicles) invalidates the prayer if uncovered, and the testicles themselves also prevent validity because they are 'awrah. 

Then the discussion turns to a person who has no means to remove impurity (e.g., no water, no clean cloth). Such a person prays with the impurity on his clothes or body and does not need to repeat the prayer later, because his obligation to remove it ceased due to inability. If a person prays completely naked while having a garment that is more than a quarter pure, it is not permissible; he must wear that garment even if impure. If the pure part is less than a quarter, it is better to wear it than to pray naked. If he has no garment at all, praying standing (with bowing and prostration) is permissible, but praying sitting with gestures is recommended. 

The commentary explains the reasoning behind each ruling. The hair that hangs down from the head is disputed: some say it is not 'awrah, but the correct Hanafi view is that it is. The one who cannot remove impurity is excused because he did his best. In choosing between praying naked or in an impure garment, both are equally prohibited under normal circumstances, but covering is better since it is less severe. For the one with no garment, standing is valid but recommended to sit and gesture because covering the 'awrah is more important than performing the pillars with full movement. 

Additional notes clarify that 'testicles' means the two testes, and that inability includes both actual lack of water and fear of harm from using it. Also, looking at the hair of a non-Muslim woman is not prohibited according to some views, though the general rule for Muslim women is strict.

Key Terms
عورة (ʿawrah): Parts of the body that must be covered during prayer; exposure of a quarter of a limb invalidates prayer.
ربع (rubʿ): A quarter (¼) of a limb; threshold for exposure that invalidates prayer.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi school, what is the minimum exposure of a limb that invalidates prayer?
A) Any amount
B) Half of the limb
C) A quarter of the limb
D) The entire limb

Key Takeaway
Covering the 'awrah is a condition for prayer; exposure of a quarter or more of a limb invalidates, and hanging hair is included as 'awrah.

Prayer with Impurity: Inability and the Choice to Cover
Ruling on inability and choice

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the ruling that a person unable to remove impurity may pray with it and does not repeat, and compare the choice between praying naked and praying in impure garment.

Core Idea
If you cannot remove impurity, pray with it; no repeat required. When forced to choose, covering is better than nakedness.

Sometimes a person must pray while their clothes or body are impure, either because they lack water or cannot clean due to harm. This lesson covers the obligations and choices in such emergencies.

Guided Path
1
Identify the scenario of inability
The text discusses one who has no means to remove impurity (e.g., no water, no clean cloth, or fear of harm from using water).
Inability can be actual (no water) or legal (fear of thirst).
2
State the ruling for inability
Such a person prays with the impurity and does not repeat the prayer, even if the time remains, because obligation dropped due to inability.
This is an exception to the rule that prayer must be pure.
3
Analyze the choice between naked and impure
If a person has a garment that is impure, they have a choice: pray naked or pray in the impure garment. Both are prohibited under normal circumstances, but the better option is to cover, because covering is less serious than nakedness.
The text says 'the best is to cover' because wearing impure clothing is a lesser evil.
4
Apply the quarter rule to this choice
If the pure part of the garment is less than a quarter, it is better to wear it than to pray naked. If the pure part is a quarter or more, it is not permissible to pray naked.
The text: 'if he prays naked and a quarter of his garment is pure, it is not permissible'.
